        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149411252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9234382450178748523">The second book of Matthew Stover's "Caine" series, "Blade of Tyshalle" is equally as entertaining and well-written as "Heroes Die". This time around Hari must deal with an even more potent array of foes, both old and new, who have conspired to completely, and I do mean COMPLETELY ruin his life and those of the ones he loves. Their collective goal is to break Caine and make him suffer to the point of despair before killing him. If they were dealing with an ordinary man they might be justified in</span>
  <span id="freeText9234382450178748523" style="display:none">The second book of Matthew Stover's "Caine" series, "Blade of Tyshalle" is equally as entertaining and well-written as "Heroes Die". This time around Hari must deal with an even more potent array of foes, both old and new, who have conspired to completely, and I do mean COMPLETELY ruin his life and those of the ones he loves. Their collective goal is to break Caine and make him suffer to the point of despair before killing him. If they were dealing with an ordinary man they might be justified in thinking they'd succeed -- but this is Caine. Never one to surrender, he begins fighting back with a ferocity that is both breath-taking and satisfying. Recommended.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6635948-blade-of-tyshalle">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8090035-dreamer?shelf=epic-fantasy">epic-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8090035-dreamer?shelf=spiritual">spiritual</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8090035-dreamer?shelf=dark">dark</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8090035-dreamer?shelf=magic">magic</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8090035-dreamer?shelf=tortured-hero">tortured-hero</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8090035-dreamer?shelf=prison">prison</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1577211784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3480536726360834400">I enjoyed the Hari/Caine parts of the story, also the parts involving the man elf Deliann. BUT there is a hell of a lot of esoteric bullshit in this 738 page book which required some extensive skimming (perhaps I'm just shallow?) As Caine would say: <i>'..yadda, yadda, yadda, horseshit.'</i><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1458211057i/18456696._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='300' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><i>"What if it's Hari Michaelson who is the fictional character? What if the middle-aged paraplegic is just a role that Caine plays, so he can get along on Earth?"</i></span>
  <span id="freeText3480536726360834400" style="display:none">I enjoyed the Hari/Caine parts of the story, also the parts involving the man elf Deliann. BUT there is a hell of a lot of esoteric bullshit in this 738 page book which required some extensive skimming (perhaps I'm just shallow?) <span id="freeTextContainer6102472765635450572">This book was just as good as the first in the series. It is brutal, engaging, and one of the most exciting speculative fiction reads of all time. I didn't give it a 5/5 rating because sometimes it had a little too much violence for my tastes, but I will definitely be reading the third book in the series when I get the time.</span>

        <a title="Soo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Soo" href="/user/show/5049442-soo">Soo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5049442-soo?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5049442-soo?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5049442-soo?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2490393273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3497571297948513515">Mix of Audio + Book<br/><br/>Note - It's totally possible to read the first two books of the Acts of Caine and be happy. The main story arc that started in the first book is wrapped up well in this one. <br/><br/>Mini-Review:<br/><br/>Woot! This book confirms it. I added another author to my favorite list. Despite the parts that are rough and bits that are not that plausible, I loved this book. Part of the story is rather abstract and there's a lot of philosophical tangents that jump around, fly and scream like hyper two yea</span>
  <span id="freeText3497571297948513515" style="display:none">Mix of Audio + Book<br /><br />Note - It's totally possible to read the first two books of the Acts of Caine and be happy. The main story arc that started in the first book is wrapped up well in this one. <br /><br />Mini-Review:<br /><br />Woot! This book confirms it. I added another author to my favorite list. Despite the parts that are rough and bits that are not that plausible, I loved this book. Part of the story is rather abstract and there's a lot of philosophical tangents that jump around, fly and scream like hyper two year olds. In an odd way, the story is very real in how it portrays people. All of the flaws, fears, motivations, excuses, relentless drive, insanity and degrees of love are carefully presented in a way that make you notice. The thinking bits are not as sharp or clear cut but I cannot help but see each piece and have my mind go off in a countless leaps of connections. <br /><br />It is not a simple story. Nor is it a happy one. Yet, I find myself coming away from the end of each book with a smile and bright hope. This is a story about a man who becomes the best of himself by experiencing the darkest depths of tangible nightmares. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_366322203" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/366322203" />
    <a title="April" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/505268-april"><img alt="April" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1191812341p2/505268.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/366322203?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="April" class="user" itemprop="url" name="April" href="/user/show/505268-april">April</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/505268-april?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer366322203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15535421206752753569">Stover’s kickass Caine character, who we first heard about in <i>Heroes Die</i>, returns in this thriller- and boy am I happy he does! To explain the scope of Stover’s encompassing science fantasy worldbuilding would take too long here, so a quick recap:<br/>-	On Earth, Caine is really Hari Michaelson, an Actor- turned- Administrator who made his acting career on Overworld, an alternate Earth-type world;<br/>-	Other Actors, like Caine, have gone to Overworld in secret, seeking adventure and using a kind of virtu</span>
  <span id="freeText15535421206752753569" style="display:none">Stover’s kickass Caine character, who we first heard about in <i>Heroes Die</i>, returns in this thriller- and boy am I happy he does! To explain the scope of Stover’s encompassing science fantasy worldbuilding would take too long here, so a quick recap:<br />-	On Earth, Caine is really Hari Michaelson, an Actor- turned- Administrator who made his acting career on Overworld, an alternate Earth-type world;<br />-	Other Actors, like Caine, have gone to Overworld in secret, seeking adventure and using a kind of virtual reality to transmit their “acting” for the entertainment of the bored higher castes on Earth; <br />-	The natives of Overworld have learned of the existence of these <i>Aktiri</i>, and are mostly pretty unhappy about it; and<br />-	Caine has repeatedly <s>fucked</s>screwed over the plans of the universe’s most dangerous men on not one but two worlds.<br /><br />Hari/ Caine is in the crosshairs of nearly everyone in power. His best friend is also his worst enemy, a man who would kill him in a heartbeat if the time was right. He is partially crippled from his previous adventures- fully paraplegic from the waist down on Overworld. His relationship with Shanna/ Pallas Ril, a fellow actor and ex-wife, is slowly going downhill. What else could go wrong, right?<br /><br />Hari wants nothing more than to be Caine again- to be <i>himself</i> again- but what price will he have to pay? Stover uses words like a weapon to tell the story of Hari-becoming-Caine: mixed in with the exceptional story is exceptional social and political commentary. That’s not to mention the sheer depth of the journey of self-discovery Caine embarks upon. <br /><br />Also, he <b>still</b> kicks the shit out of the bad guys. Did I mention he’s crippled?<br /><br />Very little is wrong with this book. The pacing is a bit slow at first, like in its predecessor, but you can count on Stover to toss in the <b>magic beans of omgwtfthisisawesomeness</b> fairly quickly. The physics of the transfer to Overworld and back from Earth is not really explained. I was fine with that in <i>Heroes Die</i> but given some of the plot twists in this I would have liked a bit more explanation. Also, the rise of Administrator Kollberg was strange for me. The reintroduction of his character was bizarre to say the least, and the things he was capable of stretched credibility to some extent. Obviously, he was a puppet, a tool for the billions on our way-overpopulated planet to use to rape the enemies of Earth <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(ie everyone on Overworld.) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> There was some sort of collective <s>(un)</s>consciousness at work in him, but piecing how that worked together was a bit tedious, at first. His role in this story was my one big complaint with an otherwise flawless offering.<br /><br />Of course, Caine is the highlight of Stover’s writing. While his worldbuilding and grasp of complex politics are outstanding, the character of Caine is, when compared to many other characters in modern fantasy or science fiction, a jewel among turds. He will make you laugh, cry, and gape in astonishment. I’ve spent some time trying to figure out what it is about him that makes him such a compelling character. What I’ve come up with is that Caine is quintessentially <b>human.</b> He is both hero and anti-hero. He holds nothing back. Caine is the spectre in each of us who forces us to look inside ourselves to say, “I am what I am”- something not white or black but complexly layered shades of grey. Caine is a simple man with more heart than almost anyone, a will of iron, and <b>giant brass balls.</b> <br /><br />I have heard some people say that <i>Blade of Tyshalle</i> was a disappointment after <i>Heroes Die.</i> I disagree; I am just as satisfied with this as I was at the end of the first Caine novel. The worlds are as rich in description and character, the action is as packed and gritty as before, and the ability of this author to write the best characters in the genre shines more than ever. Can’t recommend enough!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15535421206752753569" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1204778932" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1204778932" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8175126-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1204778932?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/8175126-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8175126-kim?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8175126-kim?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8175126-kim?shelf=gritty">gritty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8175126-kim?shelf=total-badass">total-badass</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8175126-kim?shelf=disabled-hero">disabled-hero</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8175126-kim?shelf=depressing">depressing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1204778932" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15073966155641901319">3.5 stars<br/><br/>This book alternated between being absolutely amazing and boring me to the point where I wanted to stop reading. I loved Heroes Die, but the sequel definitely has a different flavor to it. While there are many excellent Caine moments, the pacing is much slower and there is a lot more in the way of philosophical ramblings and symbolic vignettes.<br/><br/>What I liked:<br/>1. I absolutely LOVED the first section of the book, which is a prequel of Hari when he is first training to be an actor. It really </span>
  <span id="freeText15073966155641901319" style="display:none">3.5 stars<br /><br />This book alternated between being absolutely amazing and boring me to the point where I wanted to stop reading. I loved Heroes Die, but the sequel definitely has a different flavor to it. While there are many excellent Caine moments, the pacing is much slower and there is a lot more in the way of philosophical ramblings and symbolic vignettes.<br /><br />What I liked:<br />1. I absolutely LOVED the first section of the book, which is a prequel of Hari when he is first training to be an actor. It really made me interested to read about some of his earlier adventures when his life was simpler and his only goal was to "risk his life in interesting ways".<br /><br />2. Pretty much all the scenes from Caine's POV are great. I love the action sequences, his larger than life attitude, and his sneaky plotting. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I love how he foils an attempt at mind control because he can't fathom why he would kill his wife to save the entire human race (after all, he probably won't like most of them anyway). <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />3. The scope of this book is a lot more epic, and the consequences are much farther reaching, which really made the plot gripping. I've been wanting to see Caine face off against the Overworld Corporation since Book 1, and I'm happy I got my wish.<br /><br />4. I liked how the author didn't shy away from keeping Caine paralyzed throughout the book. I typically enjoy books about disabled heroes and Caine is more terrifying with no working legs than most badasses are with two.<br /><br />What I didn't like:<br />1. There was too much philosophy written in the book for my taste. I bought the audiobook and the e-book and whenever the plot started to get bogged down in philosophy and introspection, I switched to the e-book so I could get through it faster, as the audio version made it drag on. <br /><br />2. The villain of the book was a little too amorphous and too disgusting/disturbing for me to enjoy. I preferred the villains from Heroes Die where you could understand their motivations and why they were acting as they did (especially, since most weren't truly evil). I could have done without the whole 'Blind God' issue - I think I would have preferred the Board of Governors to be the antagonist with most of the more mystic bits left out.<br /><br />3. It seems mean to blame the author for creating such a compelling character in Caine that reading parts from other POVs seems dull. Raithe's POV at the beginning of the book at least, was boring to the point where I almost stopped reading (though his Caine-paranoia was hilarious). Also, while I liked Kris as a character, he was a less than compelling narrator (I could have also used less focus on Kierendal, who I never particularly liked anyway). Tan'elkoth and Avery's POVs were pretty good, though.<br /><br />4. For all that Caine's actions are mostly to save his daughter, we get surprisingly little page time with Faith at all. After all the fretting and near-death experiences on all sides, it would have been nice to get a little more father-daughter time (this could have fit in almost anywhere in the book).<br /><br />5. The first part of the book after the prequel section was mind-crushingly depressing to the point where I wasn't sure whether I wanted to continue. I'm really glad I did, but just be forewarned. I know this is intentional, since we're supposed to think of this dystopian Earth and soul-crushing, but it was rough.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15073966155641901319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_89585564" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89585564" />
    <a title="Donna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/673557-donna"><img alt="Donna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1253520622p2/673557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/89585564?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Donna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Donna" href="/user/show/673557-donna">Donna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/673557-donna?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/673557-donna?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89585564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8817215976748626850">The book opens with a bit of a prequel about Hari's time at the Actor's training academy. Then we catch up with his more recent life, working as the San Fransisco studio head after the events of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/311864.Heroes_Die__Overworld____1_' title='Heroes Die (Overworld, # 1) by Matthew Stover' rel='nofollow noopener'>Heroes Die</a>. When one of his actors is kidnapped, Hari is shown the disastrous consequences of a plot to control Overworld's resources. When he insists on taking action, the forces that oppose him set him on a path back to Overworld and his alter-ego, Caine.<br/><br/>This is a more ambitious story than that of the </span>
  <span id="freeText8817215976748626850" style="display:none">The book opens with a bit of a prequel about Hari's time at the Actor's training academy. Then we catch up with his more recent life, working as the San Fransisco studio head after the events of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/311864.Heroes_Die__Overworld____1_" title="Heroes Die (Overworld, # 1) by Matthew Stover" rel="nofollow noopener">Heroes Die</a>. When one of his actors is kidnapped, Hari is shown the disastrous consequences of a plot to control Overworld's resources. When he insists on taking action, the forces that oppose him set him on a path back to Overworld and his alter-ego, Caine.<br /><br />This is a more ambitious story than that of the first book. For one thing, the stakes are a lot higher than one woman's life and the entertainment of the masses back on Earth. And Hari's broken body is a very interesting twist. It means that Caine can't just wade into the fight the way he once might have, though some of his enemies learn that Caine doesn't need fully functioning legs to be dangerous.<br /><br />More elements are at play here, and the shifting alliances hold a lot of potential. Unfortunately some of that just isn't fully realized. Too much space is devoted to philosophical debate among the characters and repetitive discussion of the book's themes. It makes the pace feel off, but worse than that, some more concrete aspects of the story were neglected in favor of all that talk.<br /><br />For example, we never get any reasoning behind what the hell was up with Kollberg, Hari's predecessor as studio head, and why he ends up in the position that he does. Some of the Blind God stuff comes off as handwaving those questions away, despite the grander scale he is just not as satisfying an enemy as Ma'elKoth or Berne from the first novel.<br /><br />There are several other plot points that feel muddy. I'd rather have read some additional detail about those than going through so many conversations focused on turning all the subtext into actual text.<br /><br />This was still an enjoyable book and a good addition to the story. But in light of how much I enjoyed the first book, this one had higher standards to live up to.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8817215976748626850" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_620472751" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/620472751" />
    <a title="Joel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17652473-joel"><img alt="Joel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492615825p2/17652473.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/620472751?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joel" href="/user/show/17652473-joel">Joel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6635948-blade-of-tyshalle">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17652473-joel?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17652473-joel?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer620472751" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3268381532299036320">Unrelentingly dark and dismal for the vast majority of its story, this is an ugly, violent book that revels in its depictions of gore, pain, and rape - physical, mental, spiritual, and metaphorical. It intersperses its vortex of suffering with cryptic philosophical musing and characters wallowing in self pity and regret.<br/><br/>For the first 5-10% of the book, it's actually a pretty good tale of the Conservatory days of Hari Michaelson and Kris Hanson, offering an interesting glimpse into Actor training</span>
  <span id="freeText3268381532299036320" style="display:none">Unrelentingly dark and dismal for the vast majority of its story, this is an ugly, violent book that revels in its depictions of gore, pain, and rape - physical, mental, spiritual, and metaphorical. It intersperses its vortex of suffering with cryptic philosophical musing and characters wallowing in self pity and regret.<br /><br />For the first 5-10% of the book, it's actually a pretty good tale of the Conservatory days of Hari Michaelson and Kris Hanson, offering an interesting glimpse into Actor training.<br /><br />The next 80% of the book revolves around various nefarious powers spreading suffering, death, torture, rape, and more death, and the protagonists' futile, failed resistance at every step.<br /><br />Somewhere around 90% in, the story shifts a bit in tone as the protagonists finally decide to take positive action against the oppressive destruction and misery and filth that have dominated the story so far. The last few pages tease a sense of faculty and spirit for the protagonists that were sorely missing in most of the book, hinting at a better reading experience in the next book.<br /><br />Ultimately, I found TBoT to be an unenjoyable read, overly dark, self-absorbed in its philosophical wanderings, populated with almost universally loathsome characters, and only barely redeemed in the first 5% and last 10% of its content.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_170940106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/170940106" />
    <a title="Sandra Grauschopf" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2786009-sandra-grauschopf"><img alt="Sandra Grauschopf" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1266315060p2/2786009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/170940106?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandra Grauschopf" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandra Grauschopf" href="/user/show/2786009-sandra-grauschopf">Sandra Grauschopf</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2786009-sandra-grauschopf?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer170940106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15646524589062566644">As a follow-up to the absolutely outstanding "Heroes Die", I found "Blade of Tyshalle" to be disappointing. In attempting to be about huge issues like the clashes of gods, it lost its human element, and thus, much of its interest. It's a worthwhile read, but a big step down from "Heroes Die."</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_540284389" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/540284389" />
    <a title="Lark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9658616-lark"><img alt="Lark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436313269p2/9658616.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/540284389?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lark" href="/user/show/9658616-lark">Lark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer540284389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7368001115600459030">Hari finds himself in a comfortable failure. His life after his last adventure doesn't seem to have given him much happiness. And even though he "got the girl" and saved the world and was promoted to Administrator, his life isn't peachy and a happily ever after. On the contrary, it's pretty crappy the way he still fights with Shanna, the falling worth of the Studio under his administration, and especially his useless legs that makes him incapable of even being remotely similar to Caine - and he </span>
  <span id="freeText7368001115600459030" style="display:none">Hari finds himself in a comfortable failure. His life after his last adventure doesn't seem to have given him much happiness. And even though he "got the girl" and saved the world and was promoted to Administrator, his life isn't peachy and a happily ever after. On the contrary, it's pretty crappy the way he still fights with Shanna, the falling worth of the Studio under his administration, and especially his useless legs that makes him incapable of even being remotely similar to Caine - and he can't seem to change anything. But things quickly fall into action when Hari discovers a plot to create a war between the Folk in the Overworld and Actors. And though he quickly comes up with a plan to fix it all, things are never that easy and soon Hari finds himself embroiled in a war between ideals and philosophy, with blood paving the way for a new world order. It's a war between gods and every life on two worlds are at stake. <br /><br />I am actually really conflicted about this book. I love it and hate it for so many different reasons. I love it because Stover isn't scared of bringing in new characters to really influence that plot, such as Kris and Avery Shanks, but still develops the old characters we love and shows new sides of them as well. The characters were so well done. I am astounded and amazed at how Stover handled Hari post-first book because usually that is the problem with sequels - they don't know how to show different sides of the character and there isn't anything left to reveal that is interesting. Not the case with Hari. He grows and develops in this book as well. Beautifully done. Faith and Kris are an interesting surprise and also well incorporated. Loved seeing backstory as well as future characters as a result of the first book. I love the expression of Ma'elKoth and his deviousness. Characters are so true to themselves, yet still able to grow. I love it. It's believable. (high praise)<br /><br />I also love it because the plot is action-packed and not chapter is boring. <br /><br />But I hate it because it tries to do too much. This is one of the densest action books I've read because the plot skips around so much, new things and ideas and technology are introduced with a single paragraph. I don't like the fact that he just throws things in with no foreshadowing or emphasis on their importance to the story, such as Arturo Kollburg's ascendance. What the heck? Stover does not explain that well at all, to the point where the reader just must take what happened for granted. Similarly, the backstory to how Earth's dystopian structure came to be was very unbelievable as well. There was very little explanation for Shanna and the river's connection. Etc. You see, he expects the reader to take so many things for granted. Also it's just difficult to figure out what is important and what is just a supporting part of the book. <br /><br />Stover just juggles just a little bit too much - he does it well and does manage in the end, but the drawback is that there just isn't enough attention to detail for each plot twist and turn. We fly through plots without a single breather and crash into the next one at break-neck speed. The plot and intrigue with the faceless soapies, the plot with Faith, Kris's story with the Folk, the virus plot, the plot and solution to the virus, the transformation of Caine, the philosophy of Caine, the plot of the Ma'elKoth and his deception, Avery Stark's interaction. All of that one after the other without a moment to survey the world. It's all well and good because I love action, but Stover doesn't give a chance to breathe at all. No chance to process and really determine what is going on outside in the plot and also inside with the characters thoughts. At least not entirely. It's such a long book. And I notice that - which means it drags on in parts. <br /><br />There are so many plots that the resolutions seem to matter less. There is usually a climax to the resolution of subplots in stories or amazing moments where subplots converge - but in the book, only the final battle is the climax. All other resolutions to subplots just seem to fade away into a linear emotion of "plot". There is only rising action throughout the story, very little falling action (even for subplot resolution), which makes me just feel very tired trying to get to that climax. It's like a continual race to finally get to the point without a point of saying hey look this resolution matters. (Example being the resolution of the virus, or the resolution of Kris's problems, or the way Raithe meets his end desires. All of them, not resolved with a climax, but a point of just moving the plot along. Very tiring.)<br /><br />Themes. This book held a lot, a lot of themes and philosophy and just references to politics and religion. I believe you have to find philosophy somewhat interesting to enjoy this book because the story rests on that dilemma of how society and the individual should act, and their conflicts. I think Stover does a fine job incorporating these things into the story without bogging down the plotline, which is excellent. <br /><br />I am conflicted. I love it, I'm annoyed by some of it. But ultimately I'm glad I read it. It is interesting, it's worth reading. <br />Three star because I think the plot could have been tighter (it seemed to go in too many directions) so it can't exactly be a four star. I was debating three and a half stars, but there were moments where I almost felt like not reading it because I started to feel too bogged down, so three stars. But three very good stars because it is well written, a great sequel, and quite interesting.<br /><br />Recommended for people who read the first book, obviously. You can't really just start on this one. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7368001115600459030" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1264941070" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1264941070" />
    <a title="Igor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34754162-igor"><img alt="Igor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571226724p2/34754162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1264941070?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Igor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Igor" href="/user/show/34754162-igor">Igor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1264941070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3382660694551783781">This is one of the best books I have ever read. Considerably more ambitious than the first one, this book successfully raises everything to a whole new level.<br/><br/>At the beginning we find Hari Michaelson as a well-off administrator who is trying to cope with his disability resulting from his spine being severed by the sword Kosall at the hand of his arch enemy Berne. Some seven years after the events in the first book, Hari lives with his wife Shanna and her daughter Faith, who share the connection w</span>
  <span id="freeText3382660694551783781" style="display:none">This is one of the best books I have ever read. Considerably more ambitious than the first one, this book successfully raises everything to a whole new level.<br /><br />At the beginning we find Hari Michaelson as a well-off administrator who is trying to cope with his disability resulting from his spine being severed by the sword Kosall at the hand of his arch enemy Berne. Some seven years after the events in the first book, Hari lives with his wife Shanna and her daughter Faith, who share the connection with the Chambaraya, the Overworld River. Ma’Elkoth is a prisoner in some sort of the Overworld Museum, where he teaches magic, and he is Hari’s best/only friend.<br /><br />On the Overworld, the things have taken a considerable turn. Actors are still being transferred but only on free-mod and without conditioning that prevents them from mentioning Earth or Aktirs, which means that they are not in direct broadcast. The Ankhanna is ruled by Toa Sytell, and all those who are a threat are conveniently labeled as the Cainists. However, there is a new force to be reckoned with – the Artans. They are actually humans from Earth who are slowly introducing technological progress that poses a huge threat to the Overworld. The Artans and their Company are actually the battering ram of the humanity’s desire to expand and quench its insatiable hunger for resources and space.<br /><br />To satisfy this need, the forces on Earth, which share similar goals in relation to the Overworld, are gathering as a personification of the blind god, the god of dust and ashes. The studio heads, Tan 'Elkoth, and even Arturo Kollberg, are together serving this god and in order to further their goals, they must first take care of Caine. Their plot includes releasing a deadly virus on Overworld, killing Shanna, informing Faith’s grandmother of her identity thus taking Faith from Hari, and in the and getting Hari to Overworld for execution. Honestly, I cannot remember that I have ever read a book in which a character had to undergo so many ordeals. But as all those who have read the first book know, he is Caine, and somehow, inch by inch, he overcomes his ordeals and survives. Not only that he survives but in the end he is victorious. How, you will just have to read to find out.<br /><br />In comparison to the first book there is noticeable difference. Where the first book is shorter and more centered on action, the second is delving deeper into the background. There are more references to philosophy and mythology. There is a very interesting notion about myths as “stories that offer a perception of order within the chaos of existence”, stories that turn into reality. There is a whole storyline dedicated to the mythological aspect of this particular story, with characters named as the god of dust and ashes, the dark angel, the crooked knight, the part-time goddess. Two parallel storylines follow the events on Earth and Overworld, and there is also one that depicts the events at the acting academy where Hari met Kris, one of the new characters that appear in this book. Along with Kris (on Overworld he is Delianne), there is also Raithe, a Monastic official who was present when Caine killed the head of the Monastery who gave him over to Toa Sytell. He is probably the best developed character in the book. Also, I must mention Avery Shanks, Lamorak’s mother and Faith’s grandmother, another fantastic character. It would be a sin to forget T’Passe, the first among the Cainists. She does not appear much in the book but she has made a lasting impact. Her thoughts on Cainism are pure gold. It would be joy to meet them again in the sequels to this book.<br /><br />Hari/Caine starts his journey in this book as a half-broken man and gets almost completely destroyed but he never surrenders. He is never defeated because he chooses not to be defeated. That is the ultimate choice that all of us have in the end. We decide whether we are defeated or not. That is the essence of Cainism, as t’Passe explains it. It is not a religion, it is a philosophy, and as such can be applied to all religions and all people. It is reminiscent of the teachings of Alister Crowley and Friedrich Nietzsche to an extent. It may appear to some that this is too much for a fantasy book, but this is exactly my kind of fantasy – thought-provoking, challenging, well written and entertaining at the same time.<br /><br />The climax of the book is the confrontation between two worlds, two universes, and two personifications of nature’s principles. The outcome is satisfactory, myths have become reality, and reality is descending into myth. Gods and goddesses are made and remade, Overworld is saved and life returns to normal.<br /><br />In the end, we are left with Caine who returned to his true self and his true self is not to everyone’s liking. And he does not give a shit. He is a force of nature, the essence of chaos, the abyss that stares back at you and scares the living daylights out of you. He is back and he is here to stay.<br /><br />I have not been this thrilled by a book for a long time. I am aware that fantasy and SF are not regarded as serious literature, and I usually do not expect the books of the genre to deal with deep philosophical, sociological or psychological themes but when I start reading I always hope that the writer would find a way to intertwine such themes with action, mystery and adventure. This was one of those books and in my personal opinion it is a masterpiece.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3382660694551783781" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17343976434482430161">Matthew Woodring Stover is an American fantasy and science fiction author. He is perhaps best known for his <i>Star Wars</i> novels -- <i>Traitor, Shatterpoint, Revenge of the Sith</i> and <i>Luke Skywalker and the Shadows of Mindor</i>. He has also published several pieces of original work, such as <i>Heroes Die</i>, which Stover described as 'a piece of violent entertainment that is a meditation on violent entertainment'. </span>
  <span id="freeText17343976434482430161" style="display:none">Matthew Woodring Stover is an American fantasy and science fiction author. He is perhaps best known for his <i>Star Wars</i> novels -- <i>Traitor, Shatterpoint, Revenge of the Sith</i> and <i>Luke Skywalker and the Shadows of Mindor</i>. He has also published several pieces of original work, such as <i>Heroes Die</i>, which Stover described as 'a piece of violent entertainment that is a meditation on violent entertainment'. Stover's work often emphasises moral ambiguity, psychological verisimilitude and bursts of intense violence. <br /><br />Stover is deeply interested in various forms of martial arts, having trained in the Degerberg Blend, a concept that utilises the thought behind Bruce Lee's Jeet Kune Do as its foundation. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17343976434482430161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
